The missing-README friction bites only the narrower contributor/cloner audience arriving at the raw GitHub repo, not the install→fetch→login→serve→drop-in flow itself. Minor is the right severity and the lowest-impact of the known onboarding pains.

#### [🟡 minor] Three docs disagree on the recommended Linux run method (sudo vs setcap)
- **Platform:** linux · **Step:** deciding how to grant ptrace before first fetch
- **Evidence:** companion/README.md:15-19 leads with 'Easiest: sudo wfm-fetch-inventory' and presents setcap as the secondary option (lines 21-26). install.sh:106-109 and root CLAUDE.md lead with setcap ('recommended — then no sudo, ever') and present sudo as the fallback. InstallWidget.svelte:92-95 mentions only setcap. README also uses a relative './wfm-fetch-inventory' (line 25) while install.sh places the binary on PATH as a bare name.
- **Why it's friction:** A user who reads the companion README (the natural place for a Linux user) is steered toward per-run sudo, which produces root-owned writes that rely on the chown-back path and is the worse long-term ergonomic. The inconsistency erodes trust and means whichever doc the user lands on changes their security posture.
- **Suggested fix:** Pick setcap-first everywhere (matches CLAUDE.md and the security posture), make sudo the explicitly-secondary fallback in companion/README.md, and align the invocation form (bare name once it's on PATH).
- **Verifier note:** The evidence checks out exactly as cited: companion/README.md:15-19 leads with "Easiest: sudo wfm-fetch-inventory" then setcap second (21-26); install.sh:106-109 and root CLAUDE.md:121-123 lead with setcap as "recommended/no sudo, ever"; InstallWidget.svelte:91-95 mentions only setcap; README:24-25 uses ./wfm-fetch-inventory vs install.sh's bare PATH name. So real=true. But the finder overstates the impact in two ways. (1) It is a doc emphasis/ordering inconsistency, not a functional blocker — both paths produce a working inventory.json and the user is never wedged. (2) The sudo path's "worse ergonomic" (root-owned writes) is a DELIBERATE, documented, mitigated case, not an accident: companion/CLAUDE.md's "Companion chowns output back when run as root" invariant and README.md:36 ("When run via sudo on Linux, it's chown'd back to your user") both call chown_to_real_user(), so the sudo-first reader does not hit a broken or root-owned-file state. The friction is reduced to "trust erosion from inconsistent docs," which is real but mild. Severity minor is correct (borderline polish).

BUT the finder overstates that the ONLY competitive framing a JS user can reach is the noscript + one defensive FAQ. A JS landing user can in fact reach real differentiation lower on the same page: the FAQ entry "Why no Overwolf / AlecaFrame integration?" Against the genuine major pains in this flow (serve TTY passphrase failure, 5173-vs-random-port confusion, silent ~/Downloads drop, undated stale market.json), a missing above-the-fold subhead is a conversion/marketing weakness, i.e. minor.

#### [🟡 minor] FAQ tells every Windows user their inventory is at `~/Downloads` — a Unix path that doesn't exist on Windows
- **Platform:** windows · **Step:** Step 4/6 — locating inventory.json after fetch (in-app FAQ)
- **Evidence:** App.svelte:1522-1523 — FAQ 'Where does my inventory data actually go?' reads: 'The companion writes `inventory.json` to your `~/Downloads`.' The exe actually resolves the real Downloads dir via `directories::UserDirs` (main.rs:244-253), i.e. `C:\Users\<name>\Downloads` on Windows. The `~/Downloads` tilde notation is Unix-only.
- **Why it's friction:** For a tool that bills Windows and Linux as equal first-class targets, the one FAQ answer a lost user reads to find their file shows a path their OS doesn't use. A Windows newcomer who doesn't know `~` means 'home' is left guessing. This compounds the already-logged DropZone wrong-path bug: even the *correct* doc surface is written in the wrong dialect for half the audience.
- **Suggested fix:** Phrase OS-neutrally: 'your Downloads folder (`C:\Users\you\Downloads` on Windows, `~/Downloads` on Linux)'. The DropZone and step-02 copy already say 'Downloads folder' plainly — match that.

#### [🟡 minor] Companion's own README headlines `sudo wfm-fetch-inventory` as the "Easiest" run method, training newcomers into the wrong privilege model
- **Platform:** linux · **Step:** Step 2 — obtain/run the companion (a GitHub-browsing newcomer reads companion/README.md before the app)
- **Evidence:** companion/README.md:15-19 — the `## Run` section's first Linux instruction is literally `**Linux** — needs ptrace access ... Easiest:` followed by a ```sudo wfm-fetch-inventory``` block; the setcap path is demoted to "Or once" at :21-26. This is the only README that documents *running* the binary, yet the root CLAUDE.md quick-ref, install.sh:106-110, and InstallWidget.svelte:91-96 all lead with setcap and treat sudo as the fallback.
- **Why it's friction:** A first-run user who lands on the companion repo (the natural front door for a CLI) is taught to run a memory-scanning binary under root as the default, normalizing an over-privileged habit and contradicting every other surface. The known "docs disagree on sudo vs setcap" item flags that the disagreement exists; the second-order harm here is *which* doc wins for a cold-start user — the binary's own README — and that it wins for the more dangerous option. It also means the README's mental model never matches what the install widget actually did to their machine (capability-granted binary at ~/.local/bin).
- **Suggested fix:** Flip the README `## Run` ordering to lead with the one-time `setcap` path (mirroring install.sh's Next-steps wording verbatim) and demote sudo to "if you'd rather not setcap." Add a one-line cross-link to the login/serve flow so the README isn't silent on half the product.
- **Verifier note:** Evidence is fully accurate and verified (companion/README.md:15-26 leads with `sudo` under "Easiest:" and demotes setcap to "Or once"; install.sh:106-110, InstallWidget.svelte:94-95, and root CLAUDE.md:121-123 all lead with setcap). But the finder over-rates severity at "major" and overstates the harm as a dangerous "wrong privilege model." Sudo is a deliberately supported run mode, not an over-privilege accident: chown_to_real_user() (main.rs:286, resolving $SUDO_USER) chowns output back, the README documents this at :36, and the binary is a one-shot read-only memory inspector + HTTP GET (README:79-86). A newcomer who follows the README gets a working fetch and is merely told to type sudo per-run; the setcap path is still present and clearly labeled at :21-26. The damage is internal doc inconsistency and a per-run nuisance, not a blocked or broken onboarding step. Also note the GitHub-only-README path is narrower than the install.sh / web-app front door (both setcap-first), and this overlaps the already-tracked "docs disagree on sudo vs setcap" punch-list item — the finder's cold-start-doc-precedence angle is valid but doesn't justify major. Correct severity: minor.

#### [🟡 minor] Page title + meta sell a pure no-server, no-upload tool, which primes the user against the serve step that runs a local HTTP server and hands out a token
- **Platform:** both · **Step:** Step 1 (discover) and Step 5 (serve/connect)
- **Evidence:** index.html:42 title 'TennoWorth — what to sell in Warframe, right now'; index.html:45/63/72 meta repeatedly 'No account, no upload — everything stays in your browser' / 'Nothing leaves your browser'; App.svelte:894-896 header 'Everything happens in your browser — your inventory never leaves the page.' Contrast: serve binds a loopback HTTP server (main.rs:771) and the Companion tab requires pasting http://127.0.0.1:XXXXX?token=… (App.svelte:1471).
- **Why it's friction:** The product's whole framing is 'drag a file, nothing else runs.' That framing is correct for fetch, but it sets an expectation that there is no server and no token anywhere in the flow. A user who internalised 'nothing leaves my browser / no upload' has no mental slot for 'now run a server and paste me a URL with a secret in it' — the serve step reads as out-of-character and faintly alarming (a token? a port? did I get phished?), which is exactly when a cautious user abandons. Second-order: the marketing copy and the serve UX contradict each other's mental model.
- **Suggested fix:** Add one clause to the positioning that pre-announces the optional local relay, e.g. 'reading is 100% in-browser; listing on WFM uses a local-only companion server (127.0.0.1, never the internet).' Naming it up front turns the serve step from a surprise into a checkbox.
- **Verifier note:** Cited evidence is substantially accurate (title index.html:42, meta index.html:45 and og index.html:63 both carry "No account, no upload"/"Nothing leaves your browser"; App.svelte:894-896 header; serve binds loopback at main.rs:771 and prints token+URL at 779-781; App.svelte:1471 token placeholder). One minor miscite: the "Nothing leaves your browser" line is at index.html:63 (og), not 72 (twitter:description at 72 reads differently) — does not affect the claim. The contradiction with the onboarding surface is genuinely unmitigated: the 3-step landing (App.svelte:902-937) and the 6-entry FAQ (1500-1577) never mention serve, a local server, a port, or a token; the "Where does my inventory data actually go?" FAQ even doubles down with "every byte stays in your tab." The finder over-rated severity at major; downgraded to minor. Three reasons: (1) the "nothing leaves your browser" claim is literally still TRUE under serve — the companion process relays to WFM, the browser never uploads, so this is an expectation-setting/copy gap, not a false claim or a broken step; (2) serve is an OPTIONAL advanced path (listing management); the core "what to sell" value the product is sold on never requires it; (3) the serve step IS self-explained at point-of-use (App.svelte:1438-1439, "loopback CLI that ... relays listing actions to WFM. Required to list on WFM."), so the surprise is softened exactly where the token gets pasted. Against the known live-pain list (TTY passphrase failure, 5173-vs-random-port confusion), this ranks as low-priority copy polish.

#### [🟡 minor] install.ps1 'Open the trade or profile screen' auth-priming step never reaches the in-app or widget Windows copy
- **Platform:** windows · **Step:** Fetch — Windows user runs the binary for the first time
- **Evidence:** install.ps1:88 says 'Open the trade or profile screen once (forces an auth call)'; companion/src/main.rs:1678-1679 makes this the documented reliable trigger. But App.svelte:917 (landing step 02) and InstallWidget.svelte (Windows tab) both omit it — they say only 'With Warframe past the login screen'
- **Why it's friction:** Being past the login screen is NOT sufficient: the accountId+nonce pair the scan needs (main.rs:1636 cred_re) is only guaranteed in memory after a recent authed network call, which the binary itself says is most reliably forced by opening trade/profile. The CLI's own error text (main.rs:1676-1680) leads with this. Yet the two web surfaces a new user actually reads before their first fetch tell them only 'past the login screen', so a Windows user who launches WF, sits at the orbiter, and runs fetch can hit 'No accountId/nonce pair found' on a perfectly valid session — and the in-app guidance gave them no way to anticipate it. This is the in-app/widget echo of the priming step that only install.ps1's terminal output carries.
- **Suggested fix:** Add the 'open the trade or profile screen once' clause to App.svelte step 02 and the InstallWidget run hint so all three surfaces agree with the binary's own diagnosis, not just the installer's final echo.
- **Verifier note:** The cited evidence is accurate, but the finder over-rated severity and mis-scoped the platform. (1) Severity should be minor, not major: the failure is self-correcting. When the scan finds no creds, main.rs:1676-1680 prints an actionable error that leads with the exact remedy ("opening the trade or profile screen is reliable"), so a user who hits this on a valid session recovers after one failed run rather than dead-ending. (2) Platform tag is wrong — this is NOT Windows-specific. App.svelte:917 ("With Warframe past the login screen, run:") and the InstallWidget run hints (InstallWidget.svelte:91-103, which actually carry NO priming guidance for either OS) are platform-agnostic; the same omission hits Linux users. (3) The finder frames this as "the in-app/widget echo of the priming step that only install.ps1's terminal output carries," but BOTH installers carry it (install.ps1:88 AND install.sh:105) — so a user who follows the installer's printed Next-steps is already covered; the gap only bites a user who reads the web app's step 02/widget instead of the installer output. (4) The "past the login screen is NOT sufficient" assertion is stronger than the evidence: the binary scans ALL of process memory and stale login/sync URL strings often persist, so the orbiter case is unreliable-but-not-guaranteed-to-fail; the error text itself says trade/profile is "reliable"/"most reliably forced," not strictly required.

#### [🟡 minor] Rendered landing pitch never names the differentiator — reads identical to browse.wf/warframe.me
- **Platform:** both · **Step:** 1 — Discover (landing page, JS users, before any install)
- **Evidence:** App.svelte:891-897 landing header + sub say only 'cross-reference against a warframe.market snapshot ... and rank what's worth selling'. Confirmed by grep: NONE of {sell-score, liquidity, Baro, arbitrage, set-vs-parts, ducat, volume, 48h trades, patience, peak, hold} appear anywhere in the rendered landing <main> (App.svelte:888-957). All of those terms exist ONLY in index.html noscript (index.html:89-90) and meta tags (index.html:45) which JS users never see.
- **Why it's friction:** The product's reason to exist per CLAUDE.md is being 'measurably better at what to sell right now' than browse.wf/warframe.me. But the rendered landing describes the exact thing those tools already do ('rank what's worth selling'). A cold-start newcomer who already uses warframe.me has zero stated reason to endure a memory-scan CCLI install. The one paragraph that would convince them — liquidity-weighted sell-score, Baro buy-and-hold timing, set-vs-parts arbitrage — is hidden in noscript, visible only to users who have JavaScript turned OFF (i.e. cannot use the app at all). This is distinct from the logged 'noscript-only competitive pitch' item: the gap here is that the *differentiating mechanics* (sell-score/liquidity) are absent from rendered copy, not merely the competitor names.
- **Suggested fix:** Promote one sentence of the noscript/meta differentiator into the rendered landing sub (App.svelte:892-897): e.g. 'Unlike a raw price list, we weight by 48h trade volume (a 200p item nobody buys ranks below a 40p item that sells daily) and flag Baro hold/peak timing.' Make the JS landing at least as specific as the SEO meta already is.
- **Verifier note:** Severity is over-rated: major -> minor. The cited evidence is accurate (verified App.svelte:891-897 header/sub plus the full rendered <main> 888-957, and the FAQ snippet at 1496+, contain none of the differentiator mechanics; grep confirms; the terms live only in prototype/index.html meta line 45 and noscript lines 89-90). But this is landing-copy polish on a pre-launch product, not an onboarding blocker — it dampens conversion, it breaks no step. The finder also overstates 'zero stated reason to install': the rendered FAQ entry 'Why no Overwolf / AlecaFrame integration?' (App.svelte:1558-1566) DOES offer a real differentiator — Linux support, no Overwolf runtime/ads, 'same insights without that surface area' — and the header itself is privacy-positioned ('your inventory never leaves the page'). So a differentiator IS rendered; what's missing is specifically the sell-score/liquidity/Baro-timing/set-vs-parts MECHANICS. Minor file-path nit: the prompt's key-files list calls it prototype/public/index.html but it actually lives at prototype/index.html (the public/ copy is the built dist artifact); the cited line numbers are correct.

#### [🟡 minor] Landing promises 'everything happens in your browser' one paragraph before telling the user to run a native game-memory scanner
- **Platform:** both · **Step:** 1→2 — Discover then install (landing header vs step 01)
- **Evidence:** App.svelte:895-896 header: 'Everything happens in your browser — your inventory never leaves the page.' Immediately below, step 01 (App.svelte:905-910) says 'Get the companion ... Reads the running game's process memory to fetch your inventory from DE's own endpoint.' The companion makes a real network call to api.warframe.com (companion/README.md:5-6, 75-77) — i.e. data DOES leave the machine over the network, just not to TennoWorth's servers.
- **Why it's friction:** For a privacy-cautious cold-start newcomer (the exact audience the 'no Overwolf, no account' pitch targets), the header makes a strong absolute claim ('everything in your browser') that the very next paragraph contradicts: you must run a separate native binary that scans another process's memory and hits DE's API. The whiplash undermines trust at the precise moment the user is deciding whether to install an unsigned memory-reading CLI. The FAQ does eventually clarify (App.svelte:1503-1516), but the header's blanket claim sets up a credibility gap that a skeptic notices before scrolling.
- **Suggested fix:** Scope the header claim to what's actually true: 'The ranking happens entirely in your browser — your inventory is never uploaded to us.' (matches the noscript wording at index.html:96-97, which is careful to say 'no upload' rather than 'everything in your browser'). Reserve 'everything local' for the browser-side join, not the whole pipeline.
- **Verifier note:** Cited line numbers are slightly off but within the stated ranges and the evidence is substantively accurate: header is App.svelte:895-896, step 01 is 905-910, FAQ is 1503-1516. The bigger correction is severity: this is minor, not major. The header sentence is embedded in a paragraph (App.svelte:893-896) that is explicitly describing the cross-reference/ranking step ('We cross-reference... and rank what's worth selling. Everything happens in your browser'), so 'everything' reads as scoped to the ranking just described, not the whole pipeline. Step 01 immediately and transparently discloses the memory scan AND the DE-endpoint fetch in the same breath (App.svelte:907-909) — nothing is concealed, so the 'whiplash undermines trust at the install decision' narrative is overstated. The strictly-literal reading ('your inventory never leaves the page') is also defensible: the inventory file genuinely never leaves the browser tab; the companion pulls it from DE's own endpoint (your own client's session) and TennoWorth's servers never see it. It's a real wording imprecision worth a one-line tightening, but it does not block or materially impede a new user.

#### [🟡 minor] companion README headlines `sudo wfm-fetch-inventory` as the easiest Linux run while presenting Windows as a trivial one-liner — but the Run section omits every Windows first-run obstacle it documents for Linux
- **Platform:** windows · **Step:** Step 2-4 — a Windows user reading companion/README.md 'Run' to learn how to actually run it
- **Evidence:** companion/README.md:28-33: Windows run block is just '.\wfm-fetch-inventory.exe' with the single caveat 'no elevation required if running as the same user that started the game'. The mechanically-detailed 'What it does' (:60-77) and 'Ban risk' (:89-95) sections follow, but nothing covers the unsigned-binary warning, Controlled Folder Access blocking the Downloads write, or the elevated-game/integrity-mismatch OpenProcess failure (main.rs:1837 surfaces only 'OpenProcess failed — not running as same user, or pid is wrong').
- **Why it's friction:** The README is the one doc a Windows user will land on after a manual GitHub download, and its Windows guidance is a single happy-path line. The 'same user' caveat is true but incomplete: the real-world OpenProcess failures are (a) the game launched elevated/as admin (integrity-level mismatch, not a user mismatch) and (b) anti-tamper on EAC, neither of which 'run as the same user' fixes. A Windows user who hits OpenProcess failed after following the README has no next step, because the doc framed same-user as the only condition.
- **Suggested fix:** Expand the README Windows block to mirror the depth of the Linux block: note the unsigned-binary first-run warning, that the game must NOT be running elevated (or the companion must match its integrity level), and that Windows Controlled Folder Access can block the Downloads write. Align the invocation string with install.ps1/App.svelte while you're there.
- **Verifier note:** The cited evidence is accurate: companion/README.md:28-33 is exactly the bare Windows one-liner `.\wfm-fetch-inventory.exe` with only the "same user" caveat, the Linux block headlines `sudo wfm-fetch-inventory` (README:18), and main.rs:1837 surfaces only "OpenProcess failed — not running as same user, or pid is wrong" with no integrity/elevation hint. companion/README.md is verified as the only README in the repo (no root README), so it is genuinely the doc a Windows user lands on after a manual GitHub download. The doc asymmetry is real.

But two of the finder's three claimed obstacles are weaker than presented: (1) The "a Windows user who hits OpenProcess failed has no next step" framing overstates it. install.ps1:91-92 — the Next-steps text printed by the canonical Windows install path — already says: 'No admin needed... If you see "Access is denied", try an elevated terminal.' Elevating the companion to match an elevated game IS the standard fix for the integrity-level-mismatch case, contradicting the finder's claim that elevation doesn't address it. So the worst-case obstacle is partially mitigated, just not from the README itself. (2) Controlled Folder Access is OFF by default on Windows (opt-in feature), so that obstacle affects only a small minority and is closer to theoretical than to a common new-user blocker. The unsigned-binary/SmartScreen warning is the one genuinely-undocumented, broadly-applicable obstacle for a manually-downloaded unsigned Rust .exe. Severity "minor" is correct: docs-only, partially mitigated by install.ps1, and the strongest sub-point (SmartScreen) is real while the other two are weak/edge.

#### [🟡 minor] companion/README.md — the binary's own front door — documents only fetch and explicitly asserts 'One-shot, doesn't keep running in the background', directly contradicting the serve subcommand
- **Platform:** both · **Step:** User reads the companion README to understand the tool they just installed

This is a documented falsehood, not just an omission.

- **Verifier note:** Evidence is fully accurate: companion/README.md:85-86 literally states the binary "doesn't keep running in the background. One-shot," its Run (10-36) and mechanics (60-77) sections cover only fetch, and it never names login or serve — while main.rs:54-55/778-782/795 confirm serve is a long-running loopback server ("Ctrl-C to stop", incoming_requests loop). So the documented-falsehood part holds. But the finder over-rated severity as 'major'. This is a docs omission/inaccuracy, not a path blocker: the actual driver to run serve is the web app's Companion tab and the install flow, not this README, and nothing about the false line prevents any onboarding step from completing — it only seeds a wrong mental model. Real friction, but minor.

#### [⚪ polish] 'Inspect before piping' trust path points Windows users at install.ps1 in a repo with no README front door

The line numbers (109, 116-119) and the quoted text are accurate. (2) The finder over-frames the missing-README as the binding gap. The "Inspect before piping" text gives explicit relative paths to BOTH scripts (prototype/public/install.sh / install.ps1, lines 117-118), so a user who clicks through to the repo can navigate straight to install.ps1 — a README is a nicety, not the only destination, so "the reassurance evaporates" overstates it. More importantly, the far larger blocker on this exact path is the OWNER/REPO placeholder: the GitHub releases link (line 109) is a dead placeholder, install.ps1:10 defaults $Repo to "OWNER/REPO" and bails (install.ps1:24), and the repo isn't even on GitHub yet (origin is a self-hosted Gitea at 192.168.1.10:3005/prowly/tennoworth.git). #### [⚪ polish] Three different Windows invocation strings for the same command across README, the installer, and the in-app stepper
- **Platform:** windows · **Step:** Step 4 (run fetch) — a Windows user reconciling the website stepper, the install widget, and the companion README
- **Evidence:** companion/README.md:32 shows `.\wfm-fetch-inventory.exe`; prototype/public/install.ps1:13 installs the file as `wfm-fetch-inventory.exe` onto the user PATH (so it's invoked bare, no `.\`); prototype/src/App.svelte:918 shows the stepper snippet as bare `wfm-fetch-inventory` (no `.exe`).
- **Why it's friction:** A Windows newcomer who cross-references the three onboarding surfaces sees three commands that look subtly different and can't tell which is correct for their situation. `.\wfm-fetch-inventory.exe` only works from inside the install directory; the bare `wfm-fetch-inventory` from the stepper only works once the new user PATH entry is live (which install.ps1:81 itself warns needs a shell restart). The `.exe`-vs-no-`.exe` and `.\`-vs-bare inconsistency reads as carelessness and, worse, a user who copies the README's current-dir form from their Downloads folder runs nothing (the binary is in %LOCALAPPDATA%\wfminv, not the cwd) and gets 'command not found' with no clue why.

- **Verifier note:** The three cited lines exist exactly as quoted, but the "three different invocation strings" framing is inflated. Two of the three surfaces already agree verbatim: install.ps1's own Next-steps block (line 90) and App.svelte:918 both print bare `wfm-fetch-inventory`. The only outlier is companion/README.md:32 (`.\wfm-fetch-inventory.exe`), and that string lives in the README's "## Run" section which sits among build-from-source instructions ("## Build", `cargo build --release`, binary at target/release/) — in that context `.\` (cwd-relative) is the CORRECT form, because a developer who just built it runs it from the build dir, not from PATH. So it is effectively ONE consistent installer/stepper pair vs. ONE developer-facing build-doc that documents a different acquisition path, not three coequal contradictions. The finder's worst-case scenario ("copies the README's current-dir form from their Downloads folder, runs nothing") is not supported: the README never instructs anyone to run from Downloads (only inventory.json lands there, per README:35 and install.ps1:93), and a piped-installer user following `iwr|iex` never needs to open companion/README.md at all — the installer prints the right command itself. Net: a genuine but cosmetic doc nit, lower impact than claimed. Severity polish, not minor.

### `install` (21)

#### [🟡 minor] setcap is silently wiped on every rebuild/upgrade; the binary then fails mid-fetch with no proactive warning
- **Platform:** linux · **Step:** build -> setcap -> fetch (and every later upgrade/rebuild)
- **Evidence:** companion/CLAUDE.md:30-33 ('Linux clears file capabilities whenever the binary is replaced. Every cargo build --release therefore wipes cap_sys_ptrace'); install.sh:113-114 warns only AFTER a successful install ('Re-running this installer... clears the capability'); ptrace_open_error only fires reactively on PermissionDenied (main.rs:1786-1804). A dev who follows CLAUDE.md's loop (cargo build --release, then fetch) hits a fresh PermissionDenied every single rebuild.
- **Why it's friction:** This is the single most-repeated trap in the whole Linux journey. A capability that the user explicitly granted vanishes invisibly on the next build with zero signal until fetch dies. The recovery text only appears after the failure, so the user pays the full confusion cost every cycle. For a tool where 'rebuild the companion' is a documented routine (rebuild-companion skill), this re-bites on every iteration.
- **Suggested fix:** Make the failure self-healing and the trap visible: (a) in ptrace_open_error, detect when the binary HAS no cap_sys_ptrace and say 'the capability was cleared (this happens on every rebuild) — re-run: sudo setcap ...'; (b) print the exact setcap line with the resolved current_exe() path (already computed at main.rs:1792) so it is copy-pasteable; (c) have rebuild-companion / cargo wrapper re-apply setcap, or print a one-line reminder immediately after build.
- **Verifier note:** The cited evidence (companion/CLAUDE.md:30-33, install.sh:113-114, main.rs:1786-1804) is all accurate and the failure does recur on every rebuild. But the finder materially overstates the impact and mis-rates severity. The reactive error message at main.rs:1796-1804 is NOT generic: it explicitly attributes the failure to rebuild/reinstall ("re-installing or rebuilding the binary clears the capability — re-run setcap after an upgrade", line 1803) AND prints the exact copy-pasteable setcap line with the resolved current_exe() path (lines 1792, 1799). So suggested-fix (a) "detect and say the capability was cleared on rebuild" and (b) "print the resolved copy-pasteable path" are ALREADY IMPLEMENTED — the finder claims these are missing. The user is never stuck: every failure ships its own one-command recovery, so this is a recurring annoyance, not a blocker. Downgrade blocker -> minor. The one genuinely unaddressed piece is suggested-fix (c): the rebuild-companion skill (.claude/commands/rebuild-companion.md) reminds the user to restart serve but never mentions re-applying setcap after the rebuild that wiped it — a real, small gap.

The pipe path fetches the script from the website's own origin, then hits the honest abort inside the script; it never resolves a raw OWNER/REPO URL. So the primary, front-and-center install path fails GRACEFULLY with a clear next step, which the finder itself half-concedes. #### [🟡 minor] install.ps1 is piped via 'iwr | iex' but never sets/checks PowerShell execution policy, and the widget never tells the user where to even type the command
- **Platform:** windows · **Step:** Step 1: running the install one-liner on Windows
- **Evidence:** InstallWidget.svelte:44 emits `iwr ${origin}/install.ps1 | iex` as the whole Windows instruction. install.ps1 has no execution-policy handling. App.svelte:916-924 ('Run it once') gives a bash-style `wfm-fetch-inventory` with no note that a Windows user must first open PowerShell (not cmd, not double-click) and that script execution may be Restricted by default.
- **Why it's friction:** The `iwr | iex` idiom downloads-and-executes in-memory so it usually bypasses the on-disk script-execution policy — but a cautious user who saves install.ps1 and runs it (the path the 'inspect before piping' advice in InstallWidget.svelte:116-119 actively encourages) hits 'running scripts is disabled on this system'. More fundamentally, a non-technical Windows user does not know how to open PowerShell at all; the UI assumes a terminal is already open and focused. There is no 'press Win, type PowerShell, hit Enter' hand-holding anywhere.
- **Suggested fix:** Add a one-line 'Open PowerShell (press Win, type PowerShell)' preamble to the Windows tab in InstallWidget.svelte, and for the save-and-run path document `powershell -ExecutionPolicy Bypass -File install.ps1` or note that the piped form is the supported one.
- **Verifier note:** Two factual corrections to the finder's citations. (1) The widget path is wrong: it lives at prototype/src/components/InstallWidget.svelte, not prototype/src/lib/InstallWidget.svelte (prompt) and the bare "InstallWidget.svelte:44" is imprecise. Line numbers (44, 116-119) are otherwise correct. App.svelte:916-924 confirmed. install.ps1 has no execution-policy handling — confirmed. (2) The finder over-weights the execution-policy half. The supported/documented path is the in-memory `iwr | iex` pipe (widget line 44; SECURITY.md:88-89 calls the install.ps1 path the one that verifies automatically "when you use them"), which the finder itself concedes bypasses on-disk execution policy — so it does NOT fail with "running scripts is disabled." The execution-policy error only fires on a save-then-run path, and the finder's claim that line 116-119 ("Inspect before piping") "actively encourages" that is a stretch: inspecting/reading a script does not require executing it, and nothing in the UI tells a user to save and run install.ps1. So the execution-policy concern is theoretical for the actual flow. The genuine, verifiable gap is the "where do I type this" hand-holding: neither the widget (line 99 only says "PowerShell.") nor App.svelte step 02 tells a non-technical user to open PowerShell. That is real but is a common one-liner-page idiom and the keyword "PowerShell" IS present, so it annoys rather than blocks. Net: real but minor, not major; the finder inflated severity by bundling a theoretical execution-policy bug with a modest UX polish gap.

The finder even concedes this, then rates the whole thing 'major' anyway. The only genuinely UNmitigated dead-end is the secondary manual-download GitHub link at InstallWidget.svelte:109, which 404s with no guard. (2) The entire failure is gated on the OWNER/REPO placeholder, which exists only because the project is pre-release and unpublished — a condition explicitly tracked in CLAUDE.md Open items ("Pick a name + create the GitHub repo. Then replace OWNER/REPO in ... InstallWidget.svelte"). There are no "real new users today" because the site isn't published; the only operator of the widget right now is the developer, who gets the build-from-source instructions. This is a release blocker already on the books, not a hidden onboarding defect. Severity should be minor: loud-failing primary path + already-tracked release gate, with only the secondary link silently 404ing.

#### [🟡 minor] In-app + InstallWidget setcap line targets ~/.local/bin, but the only pre-release install path (cargo build) puts the binary at companion/target/release
- **Platform:** linux · **Step:** build -> setcap (Linux, cold-start / from-source user)
- **Evidence:** App.svelte:922 and InstallWidget.svelte:95 both hardcode `sudo setcap cap_sys_ptrace=eip ~/.local/bin/wfm-fetch-inventory`; but install.sh:34-41 (the only branch that runs while REPO=OWNER/REPO, i.e. today) tells the user to `cargo build --release` and setcap `target/release/$BIN_NAME` (install.sh:39-40). The two routes name different binary locations.
- **Why it's friction:** A from-source user (the only kind that exists until a GitHub release is published) who copies the prominent in-app/InstallWidget setcap command runs `setcap ... ~/.local/bin/wfm-fetch-inventory` against a path that doesn't exist -> 'unable to set CAP_SYS_PTRACE ... No such file or directory'. If they ALSO once curl-installed, the cap lands on a stale ~/.local/bin binary while they actually execute companion/target/release/wfm-fetch-inventory, which still hits PermissionDenied -> they conclude setcap 'doesn't work'. The capability and the executed binary silently diverge.
- **Suggested fix:** Make the in-app and InstallWidget setcap snippet path-agnostic, e.g. `sudo setcap cap_sys_ptrace=eip "$(command -v wfm-fetch-inventory)"`, or show the from-source path (companion/target/release/...) alongside the ~/.local/bin one. At minimum, the InstallWidget Linux note should state that the path is the install location and differs for a source build.
- **Verifier note:** The cited evidence is factually correct — App.svelte:922 and InstallWidget.svelte:95 both hardcode `sudo setcap cap_sys_ptrace=eip ~/.local/bin/wfm-fetch-inventory`, and install.sh:34-41 is indeed the only working path today (REPO=OWNER/REPO hard-exits, instructing `cargo build --release` + setcap `target/release/$BIN_NAME` at install.sh:39-40). So a from-source user who copies the in-app setcap line does hit the wrong path. But the finder over-rates this at "major" and overstates two of its three legs:\n\n1. The InstallWidget:95 instance is NOT a free-floating mismatch — line 93 immediately prefixes it with \"Installs to `~/.local/bin`\", scoping the setcap path to that widget's own (curl) install location. It is self-consistent within its flow. And that flow is disabled today (install.sh REPO guard), so no from-source user ever actually installs to ~/.local/bin through it. The genuinely-exposed line is just App.svelte:922 (the always-on onboarding step 02 note), which is unconditional.\n\n2. The \"silent divergence — cap on a stale ~/.local/bin binary while executing target/release, user concludes setcap doesn't work\" worst case requires having BOTH curl-installed AND built from source. Curl-install is impossible today (install.sh:34-41 exits while REPO=OWNER/REPO), so this state is unreachable in the current repo — it is theoretical for the cohort the finder scopes to (\"the only kind that exists until a release is published\").\n\n3. Crucially, the finder missed a runtime self-correction: companion/src/main.rs:1785-1816 `ptrace_open_error()` derives the setcap path from `std::env::current_exe()` (line 1792), so when the from-source user actually runs `companion/target/release/wfm-fetch-inventory` and hits PermissionDenied, the error prints the CORRECT path + setcap command for the binary they really ran (lines 1799-1803), plus the rebuild-wipes-cap note. The user is walked to the right command on the very next step rather than dead-ended. This is what turns a potential blocker into a paper-cut.

The widget's Linux branch (91-96) indeed omits PATH while the Windows branch (98-101) mentions it. #### [🟡 minor] Build-from-source users (the only path that exists pre-release) are told to setcap a ~/.local/bin path that the build never creates

- **Why it's friction:** A cold-start user (the only kind that can exist until a release is published) follows the in-app or README setcap command verbatim, gets 'setcap: ~/.local/bin/wfm-fetch-inventory: No such file or directory', and is stuck — the path the UI advertises is for an installed release that doesn't exist yet. The only genuinely wrong surfaces are the two Svelte strings (App.svelte:922, InstallWidget.svelte:95) with the absolute ~/.local/bin path. (2) Severity is over-rated as major. The blast radius is pre-release-only AND developer-only (the claim itself notes the only people who exist pre-release are devs building from source), the failure is an instant, self-explanatory `setcap: No such file or directory`, and post-release the curl installer genuinely lands the binary in ~/.local/bin so the hint becomes correct for the actual end-user audience. This is a minor doc inconsistency in two UI strings, not a blocking or seriously annoying issue. #### [🟡 minor] install.ps1 corrupts a fresh user PATH with a leading semicolon when no user Path exists
- **Platform:** windows · **Step:** Install — piping install.ps1 via iwr|iex on a clean Windows profile
- **Evidence:** prototype/public/install.ps1:78-80 — `$userPath = [Environment]::GetEnvironmentVariable('Path','User')` then `SetEnvironmentVariable('Path', "$userPath;$Dest", 'User')`
- **Why it's friction:** On a fresh Windows account the USER-scoped Path variable is frequently absent (all entries live in the MACHINE Path). GetEnvironmentVariable then returns $null, so the write produces `;C:\Users\…\wfminv` — a leading semicolon. Windows parses an empty PATH segment as the current working directory, a long-standing security smell, and the malformed entry is persisted permanently to the user's registry. The dedupe guard on line 79 only checks for $Dest, so the empty-segment artifact survives across re-runs/upgrades and never self-heals. The user has no idea the installer mutated their PATH incorrectly.
- **Suggested fix:** Guard the null/empty case: `$dirs = @($userPath -split ';' | Where-Object { $_ }); if ($dirs -notcontains $Dest) { $dirs += $Dest; SetEnvironmentVariable('Path', ($dirs -join ';'), 'User') }`. This also collapses the existing empty-segment problem instead of compounding it.
- **Verifier note:** Code/evidence is accurate: install.ps1:78-80 reads User-scoped Path (which is $null on a clean profile with no user Path) and writes "$userPath;$Dest", yielding a leading-semicolon empty PATH segment. Two finder overstatements: (1) Severity is minor, not major. It never blocks or even surfaces during onboarding — the binary installs fine and $Dest is on PATH; the empty leading segment is invisible to the user and the "empty segment = CWD" security risk is marginal for a CLI invocation. The new-user journey is unaffected. (2) The "compounds across re-runs" framing is wrong: line 79 splits on ';' and skips the entire write block once $Dest is present, so re-runs do NOT add more empty segments. The single empty segment persists (never self-heals, correct) but does not multiply. Also note the no-User-Path state is the uncommon case on real Windows — most profiles already have a populated User Path from Windows/apps — which further lowers real-world incidence.

#### [🟡 minor] In-app InstallWidget Windows tab dead-ends with zero post-install instruction
- **Platform:** windows · **Step:** Install (web app) — Windows user copies the iwr command from InstallWidget
- **Evidence:** prototype/src/components/InstallWidget.svelte:97-103 — the Windows branch is a single sentence ('PowerShell. Installs to %LOCALAPPDATA%\wfminv and adds it to your user PATH. No elevation needed…') with no run command, no Downloads-file pointer, no 'now go run it'
- **Why it's friction:** Both shipped installers print a 4-step 'Next steps' block after install (install.sh:101-115, install.ps1:84-94). The journey silently breaks between install and fetch precisely on the platform with no terminal-savvy default.

- **Verifier note:** The cited evidence is factually correct: InstallWidget.svelte:97-103 (the {:else}/Windows branch) is a single muted sentence about install path + PATH, with no run command, no Downloads pointer, no "now run it"; the Linux branch (91-96) does give an actionable copy-pasteable setcap command. So the per-surface asymmetry is real. But two corrections to the framing/severity. (1) The finder overstates the Linux branch as a "concrete setcap+run hint" — the Linux branch (91-96) also does NOT name a standalone run command or the Downloads pointer; its only actionable content is the setcap line. The asymmetry is "Linux gets one copy-able command, Windows gets zero," not "Linux fully guided vs Windows nothing." (2) The finder's "the journey silently breaks between install and fetch" is overstated: install.ps1:84-94 prints a full 4-step Next steps block — including the exact run command `wfm-fetch-inventory` and "inventory.json lands in your Downloads folder" — into the very PowerShell session where the user just ran `iwr | iex`.
